    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="d3fd0604005946f6a4dbf6ca5758ac0c">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '0eb059d2-f285-4e51-98d8-34e879af883c'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                     <p>Shanghai Electrochemical Energy Devices Research Center, School of Chemistry and Chemical Engineering, Shanghai Jiao Tong University, Shanghai, 200240 China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Li/Linsen">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Ma/Zi%E2%80%90Feng" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a9" data-db-target-for="a9" aria-controls="a9" aria-haspopup="true" id="a9_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Zi-Feng Ma<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a9" aria-labelledby="a9_Ctrl" role="region" id="a9">
                     <p class="author-type mb-1">Corresponding Author</p>
                     <p class="author-name">Zi-Feng Ma</p>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d69166966" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Leveraging economically viable plant bio-waste-derived hard carbon (HC) anode materials for sodium-ion batteries is logical. Many plants' bio-waste materials are used as HC precursors, but their fabrication process is usually limited by direct carbonization which constrains their large-scale sustainability. Herein, the critical role of the pre-hydrothermal carbonization effect in regulating the structure and interfacial Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism/performance of HC derived from oak leaves (OL) biowaste (OLHC) is reported. The resultant OLHC demonstrates a high-reversible capacity (378 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C), superior rate performance (272.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C), remarkable cycling performance (75% after 8000 cycles at 10 C), and adequate ICE (85%). Advanced ex/in situ characterization combined with theoretical calculations reveals that hydrothermal pre-regulation of OLHC stabilizes the spherical particles, introducing more active sites and promoting surface properties with oxygen dopant-induced defects, which shows uneven surface electrostatic potential and lower activation energy for Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption thus generates a thin layer of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>/NaF-enriched core-shell-like SEI modulation with organic–inorganic composition. This enables fast interfacial Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics, contributing to high-capacity retention and stable cycling performance. The studies offer a systematic understanding of the pre-hydrothermal strategy for the structural design of HC from plant-leaves-biowaste with true sustainability and improved performance for SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202403142-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202403142-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>With abundant global sodium resources and a low-price tag, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are among the most promising next-generation electrochemical energy storage (EES) devices for high-tech electric vehicles, portable electronics, and grid-scale energy storage applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0001_R_d69166958e389" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Energy Storage Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To realize the practical application of SIBs, substantial efforts have been dedicated to searching for well-matched electrode materials that ensure steady cycling and adequate reversible capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0002_R_d69166958e394" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Batteries">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As the anode of choice, non-graphitizable hard carbon (HC) is considered a watershed for SIB technology due to its low cost and decent capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0003_R_d69166958e399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Carbon">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> HC has a unique house-of-cards-like microstructure with a high degree of disordered graphene sheets, which provides several Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanisms, such as intercalation, nanopore filling, and surface chemisorption.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0004_R_d69166958e407" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Natl. Sci. Rev. Adv. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This makes it a promising anode material with high reversible capacity (≈250–300 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and good cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0005_R_d69166958e415" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Molecules">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The use of HC anodes for SIBs was first documented by Steven and Dahn in 2000, using glucose as a carbon source.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0006_R_d69166958e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Following that, scientists have prepared HCs from a variety of precursors, including biomass, coal, feedstocks, and petroleum products, via direct carbonization, hydrothermal procedures, spark plasma sintering, microwave heating, or a combination of these strategies for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0007_R_d69166958e425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - ChemSusChem Adv. Funct. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similarly, several battery companies worldwide, including Faradion Ltd., Tiamat-sodium cells, Enerox GmbH, Natron Energy, and HiNa Battery, have unveiled pilot projects on Na<sup>+</sup>-based prototype cells using HC anode to bring the technology to commercialization shortly.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0008_R_d69166958e433" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Sustain. Res.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Plant leaf biomass is among the most plentiful, biodegradable, and environmentally benign carbon sources on Earth, and has been developed as an energy material in academic and industrial research for electrochemical energy storage (EES) and conversion applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0009_R_d69166958e440" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Processes">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As an anode for Na<sup>+</sup> storage, HC derived from natural leaves possesses a holistic carbonaceous framework with substantially hierarchical porous (micro/nanopores) architectures and integrated channels, which can accelerate charge transfer kinetics and provide an efficient background for the rapid transit of ions and electrons.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0010_R_d69166958e447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Batteries">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the past decade, various plant leaf-oriented materials and other biomass have been widely explored as HC precursors for SIBs under various alteration and activation measures. For instance, olive leaf-derived HC anodes deliver 265.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1C with a low ICE of 52.4%.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0011_R_d69166958e455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Mater. Today Sustain.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The HC anode prepared from lotus leaves has a specific capacity of 250 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 40 mAg<sup>−1</sup> with an ICE of 66%.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0012_R_d69166958e465" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Surf. Coat. Technol.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, the HC anode derived from palm leaves exhibits a capacity of 373 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 25 mAg<sup>−1</sup> with an ICE close to 54%.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0013_R_d69166958e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Power Sources">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similarly, other biomass-derived HC, such as apple/banana peel, sorghum stalks, Palm fruit, and peanut/coconut shells, deliver specific capacities ranging from 150 to 300 mAhg<sup>−1</sup> with ICEs of 60%–75%.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0014_R_d69166958e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Environ. Chem. Eng. J. Energy Chem. Discovery ChemElectroChem Green Energy Environ.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In fact, various HC anode materials produced from biowaste have been discovered to have excellent Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0015_R_d69166958e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, when looking back on the synthesis evolution of all aforementioned biomass-derived HC, we can glimpse the general concerns of substantial carbon emission achieved from direct carbonization and acid–base treatment at different stages of development. Some of these approaches, by definition, preclude the overall sustainability and industrial-scale production of such biomass-derivative HCs because the development method is lengthy and challenging, with a meager output and unviable discharge of acidic and basic waste. To address these issues, it is essential to develop facile and rational synthesis methods for biowaste HC precursors, which offer substantial compensation for low cost without depleting renewable resources, protect the environment from CO<sub>2</sub> and other toxic chemical emissions, provide bulk output, and exhibit excellent electrochemical performance.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, the hydrothermal carbonization route is employed as a tunable pre-treatment process for oak leaf (OL) biowaste precursor, followed by post-high temperature treatment, to produce high-performance hard carbon (HC) anodes. It is important to note that oak leaves were selected as HC precursors due to their status as one of the most abundant biomass sources on Earth, with a great diversity of species found in North America, Europe, Asia, and Africa.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0016_R_d69166958e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Mol. Ecol. Resour.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition to being in a prime location to obtain the necessary precursor, oak leaves come from a class of hardwood trees with anisotropic surfaces, a low-tortuosity framework, and a higher proportion of cellulose (45% wt), hemicellulose (22% wt), and lignin (25% wt), which are highly beneficial and abundant biopolymers for the synthesis of HC.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0017_R_d69166958e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The critical role of synthetic conditions (i.e., hydrothermal pre-carbonization and direct carbonization) in regulating the structure and electrochemical Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance was evaluated and compared. Using ex situ structure analysis, it was found that hydrothermal pre-carbonization treatment can regulate and stabilize the OLHC features, resulting in smooth spherical morphology with compact packing density (0.72 g mL<sup>−1</sup>), hierarchical expanded graphitic regions (d<sub>002</sub> ≈ 0.394 nm), highly developed micro/mesoporous structures, and introduced more active sites including defects and nanovoids, which can boost overall Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance. The electrochemical results revealed that OLHC prepared via hydrothermal pre-carbonization, followed by post-high temperature treatment (H-OLHC-1300 °C) outperformed those prepared by direct carbonization (D-OLHC-1300 °C) and demonstrated high-reversible capacity (378 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C; 1C = 200 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), superior rate performance (272.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C), remarkable cycling performance (97%/88%/75% reversible capacity after 100/1200/8000 cycles at 0.1C/1 C/10 C) and decent ICE (85%). More importantly, by employing electrochemical kinetic analysis and advanced in situ characterization combined with theoretical calculations, we provide a fundamental understanding of the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism and interfacial phenomena, including SEI layer development and compositions. The findings reveal that the pre-hydrothermal treatment of OLHC can induce the surface properties of the spherical particles with more oxygen dopant-induced defects which shows uneven surface electrostatic potential and lower activation energy for Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption. This, in turn, generates a thin layer of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>/NaF-enriched core-shell-like SEI with organic–inorganic composition, enabling fast interfacial Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics and contributing to high-capacity retention and stable cycling performance. The study presents a pre-hydrothermal carbonization strategy to regulate oak-leaf biowaste and produces advanced hard carbons with true sustainability and improved performance for SIB applications, thereby implying the possible useful step toward developing a new hard carbon from other biowaste precursors.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202403142-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202403142-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202403142-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202403142-sec-0030-title">2.1 Sample Preparation and Structural Analysis of Hard Carbons Derived from Oak Leaves Biowaste</h3>
            
            <p>As schematically illustrated in <b>Figures</b> <a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0001">1a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), gram-scale hard carbon was synthesized using hydrothermal pre-treatment of oak leaves-biowaste at 180 °C for 24 h, followed by the high-temperature carbonization at 1100, 1200, 1300, and 1500 °C hereafter labeled as H-OLHC-1100 °C, H-OLHC-1200 °C, H-OLHC-1300 °C, and H-OLHC-1500 °C product for 2 h under N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere. Detailed synthesis protocols are described in Experimental Section (Supporting Information). In comparison, directly carbonized samples from the same oak leaf precursor at different thermal pyrolysis including 1100, 1200, 1300, and 1500 °C for 2 h under N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere are referred to as D-OLHC-1100 °C, D-OLHC-1200 °C, D-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1500 °C, respectively. The surface morphologies and microstructure of the H-OLHC samples were initially examined using scanning electron microscope (SEM) analysis. The SEM micrograph of the H-OLHC-1300 °C samples as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0001">1b,c</a>, reveals a sphere-like morphology with a smooth surface. The carbon spheres exhibit a notable regularity in their spherical shape, with an average diameter of 2.5 ± 0.50 um. Additionally, complementary SEM micrographs of the H-OLHC-1100 °C, H-OLHC-1200 °C, and H-OLHC-1500 °C samples (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information) corroborate these findings demonstrating consistent and repeatable spherical morphology across multiple samples. These structural characteristics stem from the hydrothermal treatment of oak leaves. Oak leaves primarily consist of cellulose (45% wt%), hemicellulose (22% wt%), and lignin (25% wt%) content.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0017_R_d69166958e569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the hydrothermal process, the hydrolysis of these biopolymer chains initially forms smaller monosaccharides, polysaccharides, and oligosaccharides. Subsequently, these compounds undergo polymerization, nucleation, and the growth of carbon microsphere in the aqueous phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0018_R_d69166958e574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Carbohydr. Polym. Res. Chem. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The post-high temperature carbonization treatment further stabilizes the shape and enhances the conductivity of the OLHC materials. Consequently, the distinctive spherical shape facilitates a dense packing density (0.72 g mL<sup>−1</sup>), thereby shortening electron and ion transport pathways (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). On the other hand, the SEM images of the directly carbonized oak leaf-derived hard carbons (D-OLHC) reveal a completely different morphology, features with lamellar carbon sheets randomly intertwined to form macroscopic channels (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0001">1d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). At high magnification, the carbon sheets exhibit macroporous channels of around 3–9 µm, which resulted from the disintegration of the flat lamellar sheet into small, plate-like sheets that entangled and form disconnected carbon block channels (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0001">1e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). In light of high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) micrographs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0001">1f</a>), the inner structure of H-OLHC-1300 °C material displays highly disordered and amorphous features, with no obvious long-range crystalline lattice stripes, indicating that it falls under the category of highly nongraphitic hard carbons domains. However, the D-OLHC-1300 °C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0001">1g</a>) sample exhibits more pronounced long-range ordered pseudo graphitic crystalline structures compared to H-OLHC-1300°C. This can be attributed to the different thermal histories of the two samples. The direct carbonization process applied to D-OLHC-1300°C provides sufficient energy to facilitate the reorganization of carbon atoms into more ordered graphitic domains. In contrast, the pre-hydrothermal treatment in H-OLHC-1300°C introduces more defects and functional groups that promote disorder, which hinders extensive graphitization during subsequent carbonization.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0019_R_d69166958e607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Carbon">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403142-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/48327944-9582-4db7-a292-71bc2b307d20/aenm202403142-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/48327944-9582-4db7-a292-71bc2b307d20/aenm202403142-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/810b95a6-9321-4699-8095-4d9340f69a3b/aenm202403142-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/48327944-9582-4db7-a292-71bc2b307d20/aenm202403142-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403142-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403142" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The Schematic illustration of the two synthesis routes of oak leaves biowaste-derived hard carbon. One is the direct carbonization of oak leaves derived hard carbon at 1300 °C (D-OLHC-1300 °C) and the other is the hydrothermal carbonization pre-treatment of oak leaves at 180 °C followed by the high-temperature treatment at 1300 °C (H-OLHC-1300 °C). SEM images of b,c) H-OLHC-1300 °C, and d,e) D-OLHC-1300 °C sample; f,g) HR-TEM image of H-OLHC-1300 °C, and D-OLHC-1300 °C sample; h) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption and desorption isotherms with the corresponding pore distributions; i) Raman spectra; j) XRD patterns and k) comparison of L<sub>c</sub>, L<sub>a</sub>, d<sub>002</sub>, and I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> values of the H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C samples.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The N<sub>2</sub> gas (77K) adsorption/desorption analysis was employed to characterize the surface area (SA) and porosity of the H-OLHC and D-OLHC samples. According to the N<sub>2</sub> isotherms in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0001">1h</a>, the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area (SA) of H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C are 49.712 and 4.914 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0001">1h</a>, displays the results of the non-local density functional theory (NLDFT) analysis of the pore size distributions. Both micropores and enriched mesopores topologies at around 1.74 nm were found for the hydrothermally treated H-OLHC-1300 °C, which may improve the material Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity, high ICE, and inadequate SEI generation during the electrochemical evaluation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0007_R_d69166958e664" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - ChemSusChem Adv. Funct. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On the other hand, the directly carbonized D-OLHC-1300 °C sample shows a predominance of mesopores with minimal macropores. This feature is also demonstrated by comparing the isotherms of H-OLHC-1100 °C and D-OLHC-1100 °C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), where the surface area of H-OLHC-1100 °C (114.099 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>) with abundant mesopores is much larger than that of D-OLHC-1100 °C (34.503 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>) with mesopores with fewer macropores. Hence, it is reasonable to assume that, under constant carbonization temperature conditions, the hydrothermal pre-treatment approach can enhance the total SA by creating additional mesopores within the OLHCs samples.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0026_R_d69166958e681" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Mater. Chem. A">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As the carbonization treatment rises from 1100 to 1500 °C, the specific SA of OLHCs declines and their average pore widths increase for both H-OLHC and D-OLHC types sample (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the carbonization temperature plays a crucial role in reducing the SA, as the most noticeable change in SA occurs between 1100 and 1300 °C owing to the majority of micropores closure at this temperature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0025_R_d69166958e690" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nano Res.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The disordered structures of H-OLHC and D-OLHC can be examined using Raman, X-ray diffraction (XRD), and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). Raman spectra of H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0001">1i</a>) display the two characteristic bands at around 1342 cm<sup>−1</sup> (D-band) and 1583 cm<sup>−1</sup> (G-band) assigned to the disorder-induced band and crystalline graphitic lattice structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0020_R_d69166958e704" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Since the HC structure comprises the lattice in the form of disordered&nbsp;turbostratic nanodomains (DTNDs), it should be affirmed that the graphitic lattice in the HC structure is significantly different from the traditional graphite, graphene, and carbon nanotubes lattice.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0021_R_d69166958e710" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast to the ordered graphitic carbons the G and D band peak intensity ratio (I<sub>G</sub>/I<sub>D</sub>) of HC increases when the concentration of defects within the distorted graphene layers reduces. Therefore, the lower I<sub>G</sub>/I<sub>D</sub> value of H-OLHC-1300 °C compared to that of D-OLHC-1300 °C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0001">1i</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) reveals that the hydrothermal pretreatment process is responsible for poor graphitization and numerous defects within the H-OLHC samples. Likewise, the intensity ratio increases for both the H-OLHC and D-OLHC samples as the carbonization temperature increases from 1100 to 1500 °C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information), implying the microcrystalline slightly increasing ordering extent by temperature treatment. Moreover, the D-OLHC-1500 °C exhibits the lowest degree of disorder, which agrees with the observation of the well-pronounced second-order 2D (2686 cm<sup>−1</sup>) and D + G (2909 cm<sup>−1</sup>) bands corresponding to graphitic structures.</p>
            
            <p>Additionally, the previously stated defect transitions can be further divided into intrinsic and extrinsic defect structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0022_R_d69166958e740" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Mater. Today Energy">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> X-ray diffraction patterns were investigated to selectively study the intrinsic defects caused by the “house of cards” structural motifs. Concurrently, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was used to study the anion dopant-induced extrinsic defects, chemical composition, and elemental status on the OLHC surfaces. XRD pattern for both H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C samples show two peaks observed with relatively broad diffraction angles of 2θ = 22.8° and 43.6° corresponding to the (002) and (100) Bragg reflections of graphitic domains (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0001">1j</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0023_R_d69166958e748" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Mater. Chem. A">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This indicates that both the carbon samples have smaller layer-to-layer-crystalline-coherency-with-wider-interlayer-spacing between sp<sup>2</sup> carbon sheets than 0.334 nm for the graphite layer. However, H-OLHC-1300 °C is more disordered than D-OLHC-1300 °C, with calculated d<sub>002</sub> of 0.393 and 0.373 nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0001">1k</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), which is well in agreement with the HR-TEM analysis results in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0001">1f,g</a>. As the pyrolysis temperature reaches 1500 °C, the (002) peaks shift faintly to a higher reflection angle, and the (100) peak intensity increases relatively for both types, showing that the interlayer distance (d<sub>002</sub>) decreases and the degree of order increases (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The d-spacing data show that even after being carbonized at 1500 °C, the H-OLHC retains a highly disordered microcrystallites phase (&gt;0.40 nm) and a somewhat ordered microcrystallites (pseudo-graphitic; &gt;0.36–0.40 nm) phase at 1500 °C. These structures are ideal for Na<sup>+</sup>-ion storage because the energy barrier for inserting or extracting Na<sup>+</sup>-ion is minimal (close to 0 eV).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0024_R_d69166958e780" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nano Energy">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>In the meantime, XPS was investigated to determine the surface chemical composition and elemental state of the H-OLHC and D-OLHC samples. According to the XPS survey-scan spectra (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0002">2a</a>) C 1s, and O 1s signals located at ≈284.75, and ≈531.67 eV was detected for the H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C sample signifying the existence of C, and O elements without any other impurities. From the quantitative atomic percentages and fitted core-level XPS spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0002">2b–d</a>), the atomic amount of O doping inside H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C can reach 10.17 and 8.08%, respectively, in which C = O (O−I) and C−OH (O−I) or C−O −C (O−II)dominated. Besides, the O − III peaks representing the RCOOH (carboxyl group) oxygen on the surface of H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C can be also found. It has been proposed that the oxygen-containing functional groups on the surface of carbon-based anodes contribute to their capacity at voltages greater than 1.5 V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0025_R_d69166958e797" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nano Res.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, when the carbonization temperature rises from 1100 to 1500 °C the O-abundances decrease for both type H-OLHC and D-OLHC samples (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0002">2b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). Overall, the results of the experimental characterization show that the hydrothermal pre-treatment method incorporates more defect transitions into OLHC, which has led to the discovery of materials with high surface properties and modulated the structural and electronic properties and the detail electrochemical characterization can shed light on the hydrothermal method role in sustainable Na<sup>+</sup>-ion battery anodes.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403142-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/606ceaa0-96eb-4123-90cd-97efb7112f59/aenm202403142-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/606ceaa0-96eb-4123-90cd-97efb7112f59/aenm202403142-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/eed21c8d-c8ae-4ad6-9ac5-b3753e008876/aenm202403142-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/606ceaa0-96eb-4123-90cd-97efb7112f59/aenm202403142-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403142-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403142" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XPS surveys of H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C; b) the quantitative atomic percentage of C, and O for both type samples; Fitted C 1s and O 1s core-level XPS spectra of c) H-OLHC-1300 °C and d) D-OLHC-1300 °C, respectively.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202403142-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202403142-sec-0040-title">2.2 Sodium Storage Characterization in the Half-Cell Configuration</h3>
            
            <p>The Na||HC half-cell construction can be used to examine the Na<sup>+</sup> storing capacity of type H-OLHC and D-OLHC samples. The H-OLHC and D-OLHC samples electrode is employed as the working electrode in a two-electrode setup, positioned opposite a piece of metallic Na which serves as both the reference and counter electrodes. The half cells were tested in the voltage range of 0.01–2.5 V relative to Na<sup>+</sup>/Na with 1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diethylene glycol dimethyl ether (diglyme) serving as the electrolyte. The initial galvanostatic discharge/charge (GCD) curves of H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C in the potential range of 0.01–2.5 V are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0003">3a</a>. Notably, both anodes exhibit a typical discharge/charge profile of hard carbon, comprising a sloping potential curve above 0.1 V and a small low-voltage plateau curve below 0.1 V. Among them, H-OLHC-1300 °C delivers the highest reversible charge capacity of 378 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C (1C = 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup>) with a corresponding ICE of 84.7%, which is the top-ranked in the H-OLHC synthesis series. Conversely, the reversible charge capacity of D-OLHC-1300 °C is 305 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a corresponding ICE of 79.6% with similar highest results among the D-OLHC series. Moreover, 69.8% of the specific capacity of the H-OLHC-1300 °C electrode comes from the plateau region (below 0.1 V) (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), which would be advantageous for constructing a full cell.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0004_R_d69166958e861" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Natl. Sci. Rev. Adv. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, the two most performance-oriented samples in each series, H-OLHC-1300 °C, and D-OLHC-1300 °C, derived from separate synthesis processes, will undergo detailed studies. Among the control samples, the H-OLHC-1000 °C and D-OLHC-1000 °C demonstrated much lower reversible charge capacity and ICE, due to extensive SEI production on the abundant open pores (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0026_R_d69166958e873" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Mater. Chem. A">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The slope and plateau capacity of each sample are differentiated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a,b</a> (Supporting Information). After being subjected to carbonization from 1000 to 1500 °C, the decline in the sloping domain contribution can be associated with a reduction in hard carbon defect sites in the H-OLHC or D-OLHC series. This suggests that the sloping domain may represent the adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> at these defect sites, which is in line with earlier findings.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0027_R_d69166958e884" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Energy Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, high plateau capacities were seen in H-OLHC-1300 °C and H-OLHC-1500 °C samples compared to the D-OLHC series, which reflected the presence of more closed nanovoids in H-OLHC-1300 °C and H-OLHC-1500 °C as mentioned above.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403142-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4e5f9ae8-df0e-4764-b020-a4e3ccd43e96/aenm202403142-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4e5f9ae8-df0e-4764-b020-a4e3ccd43e96/aenm202403142-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/851e80b7-4c73-4b1a-ade1-9f4af1e1c639/aenm202403142-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4e5f9ae8-df0e-4764-b020-a4e3ccd43e96/aenm202403142-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403142-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403142" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical characterization of H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C samples in Na-ion half cells. a) Initial charge–discharge curves in the potential range of 0.01–2.5 V at 0.1 C (1C=200 mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>); b) the rate performance at 0.1–10 C-rate; c,d) corresponding GCD profiles; e) Comparison of rate capability with the typical plants biowaste-derive HC anodes reported previously; f) The GCD cycling performance at 0.1C for 100 cycles; g) long cycling performance at 1C for 1200 cycles; h) Comparison of cycle number and ICE with the typical HC anodes derived from plants biowastes reported previously; and i) long cycling performance at 10 C for 8000 cycles.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Moreover, both rate performance and cycling tests at different C-rates were conducted for H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C electrodes. For rate performance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0002">2b</a>), the charge capacities of the H-OLHC-1300 °C electrode are higher than the D-OLHC-1300 °C electrode at all C-rates and achieve highly reversible charge capacities of 376.20, 368.28, 353.45, 339.96, 323.73, 300.04, and 272.92 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> from 0.1 to 10 C (72.5% capacity retention at 10 C). In contrast, the D-OLHC-1300 °C electrode only delivers a capacity of 302.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C and 89.13 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C with a capacity retention of 29.5%. The capacity of H-OLHC-1300 °C may recover to its initial level when the current density returns to 0.1 C, further demonstrating the electrode stability and reversibility. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0003">3c,d</a> shows the corresponding GCD curves of different rate cycles (from 0.1 to 10 C), revealing the excellent voltage and profile stability of the H-OLHC-1300 °C electrode during different high rate charge/discharge processes, indicating the H-OLHC-1300 °C electrode rapid Na<sup>+</sup> reaction dynamics. This is significantly superior to the D-OLHC-1300 °C electrode, which loses its voltage stability after 5 C. As the most promising biowaste-derived HC material in our investigation, the rate performance of H-OLHC-1300 °C is also highly competitive to other reported biowaste-derived HC materials for Na<sup>+</sup> storage (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0003">3e</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> Supporting Information), placing it in the upper echelon of state-of-the-art carbonaceous anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0011_R_d69166958e937" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Mater. Today Sustain.">11</a></sup></span><sup>–</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0014_R_d69166958e941" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Environ. Chem. Eng. J. Energy Chem. Discovery ChemElectroChem Green Energy Environ.">14</a>, <a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0017_R_d69166958e941" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">17</a>, <a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0028_R_d69166958e941" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Energy Fuels Mater. Chem. Front. Energy Fuels Chin. Chem. Lett.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A similar demonstration can be seen for the rate performance of H-OLHC-1000 °C, H-OLHC-1200 °C, and H-OLHC-1500 °C, over the control D-OLHC sample series (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8e–g, S10e–g</a>, and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11e–g</a>, Supporting Information). Following the rate performance testing, the H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C electrodes underwent continuous testing to assess their cycling performance. For the cycling performance at a low current density of 0.1 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0003">3f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information), the H-OLHC-1300 °C electrode demonstrated outstanding cycling stability with 97% capacity retention (373.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), which is higher than D-OLHC-1300 °C whose capacity retention is about 92% after 100 cycles. This exceptional performance is primarily due to the H-OLHC-1300 °C unique porous structure, which fully utilizes micro/mesopores with limited SEI formation. Similarly, the cycling performance at 0.1C of the control samples in the H-OLHC series demonstrates excellent cycling stability over the D-OLHC series, and the detailed electrochemical performance of all samples is presented in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>,&nbsp; <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information). Correspondingly, the half-cell of H-OLHC-1300 °C demonstrates outstanding cycling stability with 88% capacity retention after 1200 cycles at 1C (299.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0003">3g</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information), which outperforms the state-of-the-art HC anodes derived from biowastes reported previously (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0003">3h</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, the half-cell of H-OLHC-1300 °C with an ultra-long lifetime can run for about 8000 cycles with 75% capacity retention at 10 C (198.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>; Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0003">3i</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information) shows the corresponding GCD curves of different cycles (including the 1<sup>st</sup>, 50<sup>th</sup>, 100<sup>th</sup>, 1000<sup>th</sup>, 2000<sup>th</sup>, 4000<sup>th</sup>, and 8000th cycles), demonstrating the remarkable stability of the voltage of the H-OLHC-1300 °C electrode throughout repeated charging and discharging operations. This is markedly better than the D-OLHC-1300 °C electrode, which is only 179.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1200 cycles at 1C (64% capacity retention) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0003">3g</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> Supporting Information) and 49.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C (51% capacity retention), after 8000 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0003">3i</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14b</a>, Supporting Information). Such a superior rate capability and, cycling performance of the H-OLHC-1300 °C electrode arise from the closed nanovoids and defects of H-OLHC-1300 °C, which improve mass and charge transfer by increasing the number of active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> storage and by utilizing a micro/mesopore structure based on spherical particles with a compact packing density (0.72 g mL<sup>−1</sup>). These advantageous properties of the H-OLHC-1300 °C are a direct consequence of the hydrothermal pre-regulation, which makes it a potentially viable anode material for sustainable SIBs. Similarly, the rate and cycling performance of all the control samples including H-OLHC-1000 °C, D-OLHC-1000 °C, H-OLHC-1200 °C, D-OLHC-1200 °C, H-OLHC-1500 °C, and D-OLHC-1500 °C can be found in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8, S10</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>Moreover, based on advanced characterization, first-principles calculations, and kinetic analysis, a more comprehensive discussion regarding the Na<sup>+</sup> storage sites in the H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C materials will be provided in the following section.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202403142-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202403142-sec-0050-title">2.3 Understanding the Role of Hydrothermal-Pretreatment on Na<sup>+</sup> Storage Behavior</h3>
            
            <p>Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) was utilized to examine the SEI layer development after post-cycling (0.1 C, 100<sup>th</sup> cycles). After 100<sup>th</sup> cycles, the well-defined SEI formed on the H-OLHC-1300 °C surface features a thin layer of ±13.7–10.4 nm (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0004">4a</a>), which aligns with the traditional core-shell structural paradigm. In contrast, SEI layer thickness on the D-OLHC-1300 °C surface is notably thicker (±20.5–15.5 nm) after 100 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0004">4b</a>). The thin core-shell SEI layer on the H-OLHC-1300 °C surface can facilitate faster interfacial Na<sup>+</sup> transportation kinetics, leading to enhanced ICE, rate capability, and cycling stability. Additionally, measurements of the SEI structure on the H-OLHC-1300 °C electrode based on the HRTEM images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0004">4a</a>), indicate that the SEI shell is largely composed of a uniform amorphous organic layer with some randomly distributed inorganic nanocrystals of NaF (d-spacing 0.23 nm; inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0004">4a</a>), which originates from the dissociation process of NaPF<sub>6</sub> on H-OLHC-1300 °C surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0004_R_d69166958e1074" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Natl. Sci. Rev. Adv. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The data from the elemental composition mapping analysis of the cryo-STEM-HAADF tomography shows that the SEI formed on the H-OLHC-1300 °C surface is composed of fluorine (F), carbon (C), oxygen (O), and phosphorus (P) and morphologically features a thin layer core-structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0004">4c</a>), which confirms the presence of organics and inorganics elemental species with uniform compositions. Studies have shown that the presence of inorganic components like PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, and NaF in the SEI layer has lower Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion activation energies (100–150 meV) compared to organic species.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0029_R_d69166958e1088" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This helps enhance the interfacial Na<sup>+</sup> storage kinetics and strengthen the mechanical properties of the SEI layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0030_R_d69166958e1096" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Angew. Chem.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403142-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ee2f8ce7-22e6-4bd8-8ba4-2f5d88737a64/aenm202403142-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ee2f8ce7-22e6-4bd8-8ba4-2f5d88737a64/aenm202403142-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/08d4a493-62fc-421d-aacc-7016a6c57c41/aenm202403142-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ee2f8ce7-22e6-4bd8-8ba4-2f5d88737a64/aenm202403142-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403142-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403142" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) HRTEM images of H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C electrodes (0.1 C, 100<sup>th</sup> cycles); c) EDS-elemental maps of F, C, O, Na, and P for the H-OLHC-1300 °C electrode at 0.1 C, after the 100th cycles; d–f) Core level XPS spectra of fitted C1s, O1s, and F 1s of the formed SEI layers on H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C electrodes at 0.1C for 100 cycles; g) Corresponding composition contributions of the interphase upon de-sodiated H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C; h) The schematic illustration of the improved rate capability and cycling stability mechanism for H-OLHC-1300 °C electrode.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To further appreciate the underlying composition of the SEI ex situ XPS analysis was evaluated. After fitting C1s, O1s, and F1s core-level XPS spectra on the cycled H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C samples, the atomic composition of the SEI layers layer is unveiled (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0004">4d–f</a>). The characteristics peaks of ─(CH<sub>2</sub>─O─Na)─ (≈283.7 eV), ─C─C/C═C (≈284.4 eV), poly(ether) (≈285.8 eV), ─C─O─C─ (≈288.5 eV), and C═O/Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (≈289.3) can be observed in both H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0004">4d</a>). Similarly, the peaks of R─(CO)─O─Na (≈530.6 eV), C─O/O─H (≈532.6 eV), and Na KLL (535.8 eV) can be identified in O1s core-level XPS spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0004">4e</a>). The characteristic peak of poly(ether) (≈285.8 eV) in C1s and C─O/O─H (≈532.6 eV) in O1s spectral can be assigned to the diglyme solvent and the ─(CH<sub>2</sub>─O─Na)─ (≈283.7 eV), pertains to the reduction product of NaPF<sub>6</sub>-diglyme.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0004_R_d69166958e1145" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Natl. Sci. Rev. Adv. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The F1s core-level spectrum shows a peak of Na─F at 684.9 eV as the main reductive product of NaPF<sub>6</sub>-anion solvates (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0004">4f</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0031_R_d69166958e1156" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Batteries Supercaps">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> We note that the peak of P−F at ≈687.1 eV in F1s is attributed to the presence of residual Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>PF<i><sub>y</sub></i> and Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>PO<i><sub>y</sub></i>F<i><sub>z</sub></i> species on the surface of the SEI.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0032_R_d69166958e1177" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - ACS Cent. Sci.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> From the XPS analysis, we have found that the SEI structure emerges on the surface of H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C electrodes are a combination of organics–inorganics hybrid species. However, the SEI layer of H-OLHC-1300 °C exhibits the highest proportion of organic species (≈ 69.6 at%), as shown by the fitted XPS spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0004">4g</a>), which is higher than that of D-OLHC-1300 °C (≈ 62.4 at%). It follows that the SEI grown on H-OLHC-1300 °C should be more flexible. Notably, the SEI layers of H-OLHC-1300 °C surfaces show a higher Na─F (12.1%) proportion compared to D-OLHC-1300 °C (8.7%) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0004">4g</a>). Consequently, in NaPF<sub>6</sub>–diglyme electrolyte, a thin and homogeneous PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup><sub>,</sub> and NaF-enriched SEI can form on the H-OLHC-1300 °C electrode after cycling. It is reasonable to assume that the improved cycling performance of H-OLHC-1300 °C is associated with the fact that the core-shell type of thin SEI layer model, which is supported by the organic–inorganic phases (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0004">4h</a>) can increase the fast interfacial Na<sup>+</sup> ion kinetics and Na<sup>+</sup>-ion diffusion coefficient during cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0033_R_d69166958e1203" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>In addition, to understand the stable SEI formation and the excellent rate and cycling performance, DFT (Density Functional Theory) calculation based on the electrostatic surface potential (ESP) of NaPF<sub>6</sub>-diglyme, D-OLHC-1300 °C, and H-OLHC-1300 °C optimized molecular geometries were conducted (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0005">5a</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0034_R_d69166958e1218" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys. J. Comput. Chem. Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to the ESP calculation, in the NaPF<sub>6</sub>-diglyme molecule group, the PF<sub>6</sub> anion has the most concentrated electronegativity. Through the calculation of D-OLHC-1300 °C and H-OLHC-1300 °C, it can be concluded that in D-OLHC-1300 °C, the surface electrostatic potential distribution is relatively uniform and neutral in charge, while H-OLHC-1300 °C shows uneven surface electrostatic potential and generates a large number of positively charged parts because more oxygen groups provide electron absorption ability. The PF₆ anion in the NaPF<sub>6</sub>-diglyme molecule group has a certain attraction effect on the HC surface, which promotes the formation of stable SEI dominated by PF<sub>6</sub> anion with dispersed NaF reductive compounds and enhances the adsorption capacity of the NaPF<sub>6</sub>-diglyme molecule group. Comparing the binding energy of NaPF<sub>6</sub>-diglyme and hard carbon between H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C it was found that the binding energy between H-OLHC-1300 °C and NaPF<sub>6</sub>-diglyme (28.11 eV) was much higher than that of D-OLHC-1300 °C and NaPF<sub>6</sub>-diglyme (23.02 eV), indicating an easier process for either SEI forming and lower activation energy for sodium absorption resulting a higher rate performance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0005">5b</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). Considering the morphologies into account it's found that, the smooth spherical morphology of H-OLHC-1300 °C can homogenize the Na<sup>+</sup> flow to stabilize the SEI formation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0005">5c</a>). At the same time, the irregular morphology of D-OLHC-1300 °C can lead to non-uniform Na<sup>+</sup> flux with unstable SEI formation, which contributes to poor rate performance during the high-rate charge-discharge process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0035_R_d69166958e1255" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover from the 2ps ab initio molecular dynamics (AIMD) simulations, it estimated that the average bond length of Na─O is slightly smaller in D-OLHC-1300 °C (5.16 Å) than H-OLHC-1300 °C (5.55 Å) from 0ps-2ps, indicating easier for sodium ion reduction without forming SEI (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0005">5d</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0036_R_d69166958e1263" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Chem. Phys. J. Comput. Chem. WIREs Comput. Mol. Sci. WIREs Comput. Mol. Sci.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similarly, the average bond length between F-C was calculated to be 3.13 Å (0ps), 3.36 Å (1ps), 3.63 Å (2ps), for D-OLHC-1300 °C and 2.86 Å (0ps), 2.78 Å (1ps), 3.17 Å (2ps), for H-OLHC-1300 °C, indicating that the PF<sub>6</sub> anion moves nearer H-OLHC-1300 °C compared with D-OLHC-1300 °C indicating PF<sub>6</sub> anion is easier to form stable SEI on H-OLHC-1300 °C than D-OLHC-1300 °C.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403142-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/eb58f65a-37ff-42ed-a696-830533c9690b/aenm202403142-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/eb58f65a-37ff-42ed-a696-830533c9690b/aenm202403142-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d36af19e-7a7c-4dbc-80d9-d2d80f6733cb/aenm202403142-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/eb58f65a-37ff-42ed-a696-830533c9690b/aenm202403142-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403142-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403142" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The electrostatic surface potential (ESP) of NaPF<sub>6</sub>-2 diglymes, D-OLHC-1300 °C and H-OLHC-1300 °C optimize molecular geometries obtained by DFT calculation; b) A comparison of binding energy of NaPF<sub>6</sub>-diglymes and hard carbon between H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C; c) The schematic illustration of the Na<sup>+</sup> flow from the NaPF<sub>6</sub>-diglymes to D-OLHC-1300 °C and H-OLHC-1300 °C; d) 2ps Ab initio molecular dynamics (AIMD) simulations of D-OLHC-1300 °C and H-OLHC-1300 °C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202403142-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202403142-sec-0060-title">2.4 Insights on the Sodium Storage Mechanism through Kinetic Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>To study the merits of the electrochemical reaction kinetics for H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C, cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements in the half-cell configuration were evaluated. As shown in <b>Figures</b> <a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0006">6a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16a</a>, (Supporting Information), H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C exhibit different CV curves at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> due to their different electrochemical kinetics, that is well aligned with the corresponding GDC curves. It is generally accepted that the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) introduces electrolyte decomposition and irreversible sodium trapping on the electrode surface during the first cycle of the reduction process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0037_R_d69166958e1321" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Energy Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As can be seen, in the CV profiles from the first cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0006">6a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16a</a>, Supporting information), the cathodic peak intensity (from 0.5 to 0.9 V) for the H-OLHC-1300 °C is obviously lower than that of D-OLHC-1300 °C. It confirms that the H-OLHC-1300 °C electrode has a good sodiation process and excellent electrochemical reversibility.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403142-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/43edd3e6-a867-413d-b531-08e49885cef5/aenm202403142-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/43edd3e6-a867-413d-b531-08e49885cef5/aenm202403142-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d60428e2-d983-4404-a9f1-3a3c61806c89/aenm202403142-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/43edd3e6-a867-413d-b531-08e49885cef5/aenm202403142-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403142-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403142" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Cyclic voltammograms of the first five cycles of the H-OLHC-1300 °C at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>; b) CV curves of the H-OLHC-1300 °C under various sweep rates; c) CV of H-OLHC-1300 °C with the capacitive contribution at 0.4 mV s<sup>−1</sup>; d) normalized contribution ratio of capacitive capacities at various scan rates for H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C; e) The linear relationship of anodic and cathodic log (peak current) with the log (scan rate) of H-OLHC-1300 °C for calculating the exponent (b values) in half cells; f) GITT profile, and calculated <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/2d3ba8c4-f759-4b61-a284-cbf4de18d3ac/aenm202403142-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202403142:aenm202403142-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/aenm202403142-math-0001.png">
                           <semantics>
                              <mrow>
                                 <msubsup>
                                    <mi mathvariant="bold-italic">D</mi>
                                    <mi mathvariant="bold-italic">Na</mi>
                                    <mo>+</mo>
                                 </msubsup>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
                                 </mrow>
                              </mrow>
                              <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\bm{D}}_{{\bm{Na}}}^ + ($</annotation>
                           </semantics></math> inset) of H-OLHC-1300 °C at different voltages with a pulse current at 0.1C for 10 min followed by an interval for 2 h; g,h) Nyquist plots of the EIS data for the H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C before and after different cycles; i) Equivalent circuit model used for fitting the H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C electrodes Nyquist plots in Na half-cell system; j) Histogram of the R<sub>s</sub>, R<sub>SEI</sub>, and R<sub>ct</sub>, of H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C before cycle and after 50<sup>th</sup>, 100<sup>th</sup>, and 500<sup>th</sup> cycles at 1C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To fully understand the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism CV measurements from 0.1 to 1.0 mVs<sup>−1</sup> can be employed. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0006">6b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16b</a>, (Supporting information), with the increase in sweep rates, D-OLHC-1300 °C exhibits a greater pronounced redox peak shift and CV curves polarization than H-OLHC-1300 °C. At a scan rate of 1.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, the CV curve of H-OLHC-1300 °C still shows distinct redox peaks, while redox peaks of D-OLHC-1300 °C become broader and gradually fade. From the kinetic point of view, the total charge stored could result from a two-step mechanism: The behavior of Na<sup>+</sup> insertion between interlayers in the plateau region (below 0.1V) of the GCD profiles corresponds to the diffusion-controlled process with slower kinetics. In contrast, the Na<sup>+</sup> absorption on surface-active sites in the sloping region (above 0.1V) was found to be a pseudocapacitive process with faster kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0038_R_d69166958e1405" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Mater. Today">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0006">6c</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16c</a> (Supporting Information), the general voltage profile for the capacitive current (pink color zone) in contrast to the total current, which is obtained at a scan rate of 0.4 mV s<sup>−1</sup> based on the power-law relationship equation calculation <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). As summarized in (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0006">6d</a>), the diffusion-controlled reactions in the plateau region contribute the majority of the capacity (≈75/68%) at a low scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> for H-OLHC-1300 °C and D-OLHC-1300 °C, which is a common trait of HC anodes employed in high-energy-density batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403142-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403142-bib-0039_R_d69166958e1427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Nano">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The contribution of capacitive-controlled processes in H-OLHC-1300 °C gradually increases from 35.8 to 81.1% as the scan rate increases from 0.1 to 1.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, whereas in D-OLHC-1300 °C it expands fast from 28.8 to 91.3% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0006">6d</a>). This trend might be associated with the lower rate performance of D-OLHC-1300 °C, which has already been mentioned, as well as the more noticeable disappearances of D-OLHC-1300 °C redox peaks at higher scan rates.</p>
            
            <p>Moreover, the measured <i>b</i>-values of the cathodic and anodic peaks of H-OLHC-1300 °C are 0.69 and 0.74, respectively, based on Equations <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information); these values are consistently higher than those of D-OLHC-1300 °C (0.57 and 0.62), as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403142-fig-0006">6e</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16d</a> (Supporting Information). As a result, the diffusion-controlled reactions kinetics in H-OLHC 1300 °C are faster than those in D-OLHC 1300 °C, which accounts for the higher rate performance of H-OLHC-1300 °C. All things considered, the hydrothermal pre-carbonization of H-OLHC-1300 °C results in increased surface and diffusion-controlled capacities, by regulating spherical structure with more active sites, that enable excellent electrochemical kinetics by facilitating the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> and electrons.</p>
            
            <p>To further understand the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/f0d8471b-d527-496f-bc5d-b4a61379e0be/aenm202403142-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202403142:aenm202403142-math-0002" display="inline" location="graphic/aenm202403142-math-0002.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
                        <msubsup>
                           <mi>D</mi>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi>N</mi>
                              <mi>a</mi>
                           </mrow>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msubsup>
                     </mrow>
